    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2056886318" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2056886318" />
    <a title="jenny✨" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5160409-jenny"><img alt="jenny✨" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534561275p2/5160409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2056886318?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jenny✨" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jenny✨" href="/user/show/5160409-jenny">jenny✨</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to jenny✨ by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/87993607-claude-s-bookzone">Claude&#39;s Bookzone</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=spoopy-szn-2020">spoopy-szn-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=badass-heroine">badass-heroine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=books-i-loved">books-i-loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=the-walking-dead">the-walking-dead</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=ya-dystopia">ya-dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2056886318" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16877505145506483027"><i>
  <blockquote>Not all the magic of the earth is benevolent.</blockquote>
</i><br/><b>This came highly recommended to me by several Goodreads friends, and it did. not. disappoint.</b><br/><br/>In fact, I would say that this is some of the best zombie fiction I’ve read in a long while, and I’ve read a decent amount. (I am a SUCKER for stories set amidst the zombie apocalypse. There’s just something about the undead that really gets under my skin in a creepy, catch-hold-of-my-interest-and-never-let-it-go kind of way.)<br/><br/>The writing in this book is a </span>
  <span id="freeText16877505145506483027" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>Not all the magic of the earth is benevolent.</blockquote>
</i><br /><b>This came highly recommended to me by several Goodreads friends, and it did. not. disappoint.</b><br /><br />In fact, I would say that this is some of the best zombie fiction I’ve read in a long while, and I’ve read a decent amount. (I am a SUCKER for stories set amidst the zombie apocalypse. There’s just something about the undead that really gets under my skin in a creepy, catch-hold-of-my-interest-and-never-let-it-go kind of way.)<br /><br />The writing in this book is a blend of Temple’s colloquial commentary and descriptive passages made up of beautiful prose. I found the combination to be utterly compulsively readable, and devoured this novel in the span of a day. <br /><br />I’m also rethinking my shelving of this book as “young adult.” Apart from the narrator’s age (fifteen), I don’t really see anything distinguishably YA about it; the prose, its cadence, could rival that of literary fiction.<br /><br />Needless to say, I LOVED IT.<br /><br />◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>Doggone it, she says. Why do livin and dyin always have to be just half an inch apart?</blockquote>
</i><br /><i>The Reapers Are the Angels</i> tackles that age-old question, one that especially haunts post-apocalyptic settings: What is good? What is evil? How does one exist within the amorphous bounds of grey morality and mortality when the world has gone to shit? Can we retain our humanity <i>and</i> survive? <br /><br />It’s easy to fall into trite territory with these questions, seeing as they’ve been explored so many times over. BUT the opposite is true as well—as a function of their vastness, the questions can be answered innumerable ways. And this book took an approach that was made particularly compelling by its conflicted yet resolute heroine. <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>Stayin alive ain’t the hard part. The problem is stayin right.</blockquote>
</i><br />In terms of undead world-building, several elements of Temple’s dystopia really struck me as novel, even among the many zombie tropes I’ve read/watched over the years. First, her zombies can turn cannibal and devour each other when they get too hungry. Second… there’s some zombie x human intermingling that reminded me of <i>Z Nation</i>—and it was pretty freakin’ <s>squicky</s> awesome. <br /><br />The reason why my rating is 4 stars and not 5 is because I found a couple things a smidge unbelievable. There seemed to be a ready and easily accessible abundance of supplies—food, weapons, ammo, cars, GAS—any time Temple needed anything. Also, everyone was so damn <i>trusting!</i> Disbelievingly so! Just about every community Temple encounters welcomes her and others in with barely a suspicious thought. <br /><br />Me? I’d be paranoid as hell. <br /><br />Because OPEN TRUST is how one gets KILLED DEAD in a zombie apocalypse!!!<br /><br />If there’s anything I’ve learned across seven seasons of <i>The Walking Dead</i>, it’s that living, breathing humans are way scarier than (un)dead ones. And while <i>The Reapers Are the Angels</i> didn’t play up the backstabby aspect of the apocalypse per se, it <i>did</i> offer up some grotesque ways in which humans can be atrocious (see the aforementioned zombie x human hybridity). <br /><br />And through Temple’s eyes, we readers see the dead not as monsters so much as other organisms who cohabit the ruined earth, albeit ones who are driven by the instinctual, unstoppable drive to devour flesh. <br /><br />◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️<br /><br />Finally, I MUST write a bit about my favourite part of this book, which was Temple. <br /><br />Temple’s the sort of compassionate realist / resilient survivor / philosophical scavenger that I’d want to emulate, should we ever, god forbid, become besieged by a pandemic of the undead. She’s no-nonsense and practical, yet also has a clear-eyed sense of humour that both acknowledges and lightens the bleakness of her dystopian world.<br /><br />What I found most compelling about Temple was the savvy set of principles she adheres to: she’ll kill a man if she has to, she’ll protect others even when she doesn’t, but above all she knows how to take care of herself. She moves through this blighted world with an indefatigable drive to just <i>be</i>. You won’t catch Temple breaking down into any sort of pandemic-induced existential crisis anytime soon.<br /><br />I mean, just listen to this:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>She’s no wet nurse, no righteous savior of meek men. She knows where she belongs—with the cannibals and the madmen, with the eaters of flesh and the walkers of a blight land, with the abominations. She’s done things that mark her forever, as good as a brand on her forehead—and her denial of them would be fruitless. It would be vanity.</blockquote>
</i><br />HOW COULD ONE NOT RESPECT THE HELL OUT OF THIS GIRL?<br /><br /><b>If I were to meet this fifteen-year-old, kukri-equipped girl on the road, I doubt we’d be friends. But I know we’d help each other survive somehow, and I’d leave her feeling a healthy dose of admiration.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="16877505145506483027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('e3u7euAekdX3Ng4iPtPHIdvPo2ZeQr/yh5+1AlA2rBNJreKxi1dXJMeekCeCzXwxzgSPR7AR8mPR9sOyLN78rw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1vvz2xRuNiayXO5LtVGquFDYcqO8ij32xEmVukJfrY3kLaoQfyfw14L0cE4JTxGoRRNeglLZcGeSIOMKPrf9MQ==')})
  }

Mary Wept Over the Feet of Jesus is the retelling in comics form of nine biblical stories that present Chester Brown\'s fascinating and startling thesis about biblical representations of prostitution. Brown weaves a connecting line between Bathsheba, Ruth, Rahab, Tamar, Mary of Bethany, and the Virgin Mother. He reassesses the Christian moral code by examining the cultural implications of the Bible\'s representations of sex work.\n\nMary Wept Over the Feet of Jesus is a fitting follow-up to Brown\'s sui generis graphic memoir Paying for It, which was reviewed twice in The New York Times and hailed by sex workers for Brown\'s advocacy for the decriminalization and normalization of prostitution. Brown approaches the Bible as he did the life of Louis Riel, making these stories compellingly readable and utterly pertinent to a modern audience. In classic Chester Brown fashion, he provides extensive handwritten endnotes that delve into the biblical lore that informs Mary Wept Over the Feet of Jesus.<\/span>\n  <a <span id="freeTextContainer10277362999781516770">Alden Bell is a pseudonym for Joshua Gaylord, whose first novel, Hummingbirds, was released in Fall '09. He teaches at a New York City prep school and is an adjunct professor at The New School. He lives in New York City with his wife, the Edgar Award-winning mystery writer, Megan Abbott.</span>
